4.4.3 Carbonized natural materials. Carbonized materials can be directly obtained from natural sources through high-temperature sintering and are advantageous due to their low-cost, versatile microstructures and high chemical/thermal stabilities. Mei et al.410 reported the pioneering work of using a porous carbon film as an efficient and stable hole collection layer in PSCs. Carbon-based hole collection materials also exhibit good air/water stability411–413 and high compatibility for easy manufacturing such as doctor-blading,414 screen printing,415 and electrospray416 printing. These attributes make them promising materials for anodes in PSC devices and their extended applications in MPS. Particularly from the cost perspective, carbon-based PSCs could significantly reduce the levelized cost of electricity (LCOE) of the PV MPS in contrast to those using metal electrodes.417

Compared with the synthesis of existing state-of-the-art charge collection materials which requires tedious and complex processing and expensive precursors, carbonized natural organics with a delicate microstructure are good alternatives. For example, activated carbon with microstructures (e.g., hierarchical porous structure) for supercapacitor applications can be synthesized from natural corncob418 and lignin of wood.419 For applications in Li-ion batteries, hierarchically porous nitrogen-rich carbon as an anode material has been derived from wheat straw.420 Similarly in PSCs, using an ancient Indian method, Mali et al.421 designed an ecofriendly route to extract cross-linked carbon nanoparticles from Aloe vera as the HTL and electrode to achieve printable PSCs, as schematically illustrated in Fig. 20a. The authors extracted Aloe vera gel from leaves and dried it in sunlight to obtain the intermediate carbon powder. After being washed with acid, the product was annealed at a high temperature (1000 °C). The final product was crosslinked carbon nanoparticles with uniform size (denoted as AV-C) (Fig. 20a(i)). The screen-printed carbon film from the AV-C carbon paste showed outstanding conductivity (∼7 Ω cm2) compared to one made from a commercial colloidal carbon paste (∼80 Ω cm2). With the AV-C carbon, a fully printable triple-layered mesoporous scaffold consisting of a TiO2/ZrO2/AV-C based PSC showed a PCE of 12.75% (Fig. 20a(ii)), higher than that of the device using commercial carbon ink (10.53%). In addition, an unencapsulated device based on AV-C showed a small PCE decrease after 900 hours in ambient air with a relative humidity of 35% (while the reference PSC fails after 100 hours of operation) (Fig. 20a(iii)). Gao et al.422 also synthesized various bio-carbon materials through pyrolysis of common plant waste including corn stalk (CS), peanut shell (PS), Phragmites australis (PA) and bamboo chopsticks (BC) (Fig. 20b). Results demonstrated that all of the aforementioned carbon materials could serve as the HTL/electrode layer for PSCs (Fig. 20b(i) and (ii)). The cell based on biocarbon extracted from BC exhibited the best performance compared to the others with a PCE of 10.30%. It should also be noted that the cell based on the biocarbon exhibited exceptional stability compared with the conventional PSC, and the BC-based cell exhibited nearly 90% of the PCE retained after 2000 hours in ambient conditions with a 30% relative humidity (RH) (while the reference device failed after 300 hours) (Fig. 20b(iii)).


Fig. 20 Carbonized natural materials for charge transportation in perovskite solar cells. (a) (i) Schematic illustration of the preparation process of Aloe vera (AV) cross-linked carbon nanoparticles via an ancient Indian method. (ii) Cross-sectional SEM image of a perovskite solar cell device based on the AV-carbon electrode. (iii) Comparison of the air stability of unencapsulated conventional spiro-MeOTAD and AV-carbon PSCs at a 35% relative humidity, adapted with permission from ref. 421, Copyright 2018 American Chemical Society. (b) (i) HOMO level diagram of various bio-carbon electrodes. (ii) Stability tests of perovskite solar cells with various bio-carbon electrodes at a 30% relative humidity. (iii) Cross-sectional SEM image of a perovskite solar cell with the bio-carbon electrode, adapted with permission from ref. 422, Copyright 2020 Elsevier.

Overall, the carbon-based hole collector layer in the PSC enables the exemption of the expensive HTL and could further reduce the cost because of the replacement of noble Au with carbon. In addition, carbon and its derivatives as highly conductive charge collectors are abundant in nature as most organics are carbon-based materials able to be carbonized through various established techniques. Employment of these natural carbonized materials still needs to follow the principles of (i) excellent electrical conductivity and charge carrier mobility, (ii) uniform microstructures with minimized pores, traps and defects, (iii) small and uniform particle size as well as good and dense film morphology, (iv) high purity and stability, and (v) good compatibility to up-scaling manufacturing, in addition to their unique (vi) ability to inherit microstructures from their original natural carbonate sources. In particular, application in perovskite solar cells, carbon electrodes or carbonized CTL with an extremely low manufacturing cost is one of the biggest thrusts for their commercialization. Utilization of already carbonized or direct use of carbon electrodes has been reported. The use of carbonate sources in vitro, followed by post-carbonization, in order to further simplify the manufacturing may offer additional potential for innovative device fabrication.

5. Perovskite photoactive layer

The perovskite photoactive layer in PSCs is responsible for photon absorption and a subsequent quick conversion into a free electron and a hole, thanks to its low exciton binding energy, so room-temperature thermal heat can separate the excitonic electron–hole pair into free charges. To maximize the photovoltaic efficiency in these photoactive layers, properties including (i) efficient photon absorption (e.g., broad spectral response, high light-distinction coefficient, long lifetime of excited states, clean band structure with minimized density of shallow trap-states or in-band traps) and (ii) efficient excited charge transport towards corresponding electrodes (e.g., high electron and hole mobilities to ensure less potential energy loss and charge density loss during transit, ambipolar transport ability that enables opposite drifting of excess photon-excited electrons and holes under external electric fields). These requirements are exactly met by perovskite materials, which have a good band structure (direct bandgap and a high optical absorption coefficient of 105 cm−1), tunable energy level (bandgap ranges from 1.15 to 3.06 eV), a long exciton diffusion length (1 μm in polycrystalline films and 100s μm in single crystals), sharp DOS at the VBM and CBM and low effective masses, and high electron and hole mobilities.17–21 This makes the perovskite the current optimal platform for PV applications. In addition to this, PSCs with a specific type of production will need additional attributes. For example, for PV-MPS applications, PSCs may require high mechanical properties (e.g., flexibility for curved UAV wings, fabrics and other wearable implementations), higher stability and efficiencies for outdoor applications (e.g., PV-tile roof under extreme weather conditions), and some other specific features (e.g., transparency for the PV-window; high vacuum/pressure endurance for spatial application). Under these considerations, bio-inspired strategies (including delicate structures, chemically functional biomolecules and their hybrids with the target materials)423 can be helpful in adding new functionalities to the current baseline PSC platforms. For more specific examples in terms of mechanical properties, natural materials with sophisticated microstructures (such as columns of circular layers in wood or bone, or the complex helicoidal chitin fibers in the stomatopod club) can be simultaneously light-weight, strong, flexible and tough; some of them can be porous with a hierarchical design to provide paths for efficient mass transport and/or to reduce weight; many natural materials are also able to self-repair repeatedly without any external interference; such natural materials can also be built with a relatively limited number of components and a simple structure under minimized energy supply in a highly efficient manner. Finding the common design themes among them and developing a novel bio-inspired paradigm is hence reliable, some of which have already been successfully applied in different fields.424 In terms of PVs, these bio-inspired strategies for enhancing the original properties or adding new attributes to current perovskite photoactive layers are expected to expand the horizon of the development of PSC-based MPS.

5.1 Biomimetic architecture

5.1.1 Nacre. Enhancing the mechanical properties (toughness, bendability, etc.) of PSCs is important, particularly with consideration of portable, foldable and mobile power applications. Natural hybrid materials such as nacre and bone exhibit remarkable toughness and strength. Fig. 21a and b summarize and compare the mechanical properties between natural and artificial materials. In fact, most natural materials are composites of certain forms, including polymeric (e.g., polysaccharides, proteins) and ceramic (e.g., silica, calcium salts) components or composite building blocks.425–429 From this toolbox, a wide range of hybrid materials (such as wood, bamboo and palm, comprising cellulose fibers within a lignin–hemicellulose matrix; insect cuticle, comprising chitin in a protein matrix) are assembled materials. Similarly for nacre, its hybrid composite design gives the primary strengthening and toughening mechanisms.430 More specifically, nacre has a ‘brick and mortar’ micro-composite structure (Fig. 21c). The bricks are crystalline aragonite (CaCO3) platelets with a dimension of 0.5 μm thickness and 8–10 μm width, providing strength, while the mortar (20–50 nm thick) is a biomolecular adhesive composed of proteins and chitin, providing toughness and ductility.431 This alternative laminar multilayer structure has multiple toughening mechanisms including crack bridging, pulling/sliding of aragonite platelets, crack propagation deflection and energy dissipation by the organic layer,432,433 which are well discussed in prior reviews.424,434 Such a hierarchical structure from nacre has inspired the development of engineered materials in PSCs with remarkable toughness and mechanical strength. In PSCs, one of the major challenges is the brittle nature of the halide perovskite film. It is difficult for the perovskite material to meet the stretchability, twistability and bendability requirements of the PSC device and the final MPS products able to accommodate the complex human body movements.105,435,436 Inspired by the promising results for the biomimicry study of nacre in other electronically applied materials (e.g., interface-reinforced graphene-based fibers,437 reduced graphene oxide–poly(acrylic acid) nanocomposites71), researchers attempt to adopt the nacre concept to enhance the reliability of mechanically flexible PSCs. Hu et al.106 mimicked the natural mineralization process of nacre by introducing two polymers of poly(styrene-co-butadiene) (SBS) and polyurethane (PU) into the perovskite (Fig. 21d) to modulate the mechanical features of the perovskite photoactive layer. The SBS serves as a scaffold that reduces the nucleation energy barrier and induces heterogeneous nucleation of the perovskite. Meanwhile, PU undergoes chemical interactions with the perovskite precursors which suppress the nucleation rate. Mediated by the synergistic effect of SBS and PU, the resultant photoactive film exhibits oriented grains. Owing to the stress release effect by the elastic polymeric matrix, the PSC exhibits outstanding bendability and stretchability (Fig. 21e). The SBS–PU based PSC module shows no clear PCE decrease after bending in 2 mm curvature for 5000 cycles compared to an 80% PCE loss in a reference device.
Fig. 21 Biomimetic architecture of the perovskite layer. (a) Ashby plot of the specific values of strength and stiffness (or Young's modulus) for various natural and synthetic materials. (b) Young's modulus and fracture toughness of various biomaterials as well as synthetic materials. (c) The hierarchical structure of nacre, adapted with permission from ref. 424, Copyright 2014 Springer Nature. (d) Scheme showing the nacre-inspired wearable solar module and microstructure for the perovskite layer consisting of soluble PU (blue) and insoluble SBS (orange) matrices. (e) Photo image and measured I–V curve of the wearable solar module constructed with the nacre-inspired perovskite layer, adapted with permission from ref. 106, Copyright 2019 The Royal Society of Chemistry.
5.1.2 Other promising structures. There are a wide range of potential natural examples for mimetic implementation in PSCs. These delicate structures can be applied in designing mechanically qualified PSCs and their terminal MPS products. Mechanically, beyond the aforementioned specific strength, stiffness, and Young's modulus in Fig. 21b, another important figure-of-merit is the specific energy absorption (SEA), which defines the absorbed energy by a structure per unit mass. The SEA can be calculated from the force–displacement (F–D) characteristic (exemplified in Fig. 22a) of the target material using the equation of SEA(d) = (EA(d))/m, where d is the displacement, EA is the energy absorbed by the material as represented by the shaded area under the F–D curve, and m is the mass of the target material. Similarly, SEA per unit volume (SEAV) defining the energy absorbed per unit volume can also be calculated using the equation of SEAV(d) = (EA(d))/V, where V is the volume of the energy absorber. Fig. 22b shows the SEAV of various biostructures with respect to conventional (simple artificial) structures. Among them, the honeycomb-like sandwich architecture has been widely observed in nature, such as turtle shells and beetle forewings (Fig. 22c) and has been utilized to design strengthened carbon fiber reinforced plastic (CFRP) panels438 with greater energy absorption and a lower peak load for construction applications. Similarly, the elson (tail plate) of Stomatopoda (mantis shrimp)439 (Fig. 22d) exhibits excellent mechanical impact resistance because of its special structure, which has been mimicked for designing corrugated panels. The augmentation of the impact resistance comes from the significantly improved crashworthiness and reduced initial peak force in these bio-inspired sandwich panels.440 Another candidate for designing the bio-inspired structures for mechanical energy absorption is the head of a woodpecker.441–443 Several studies in mechanical engineering have been done to mimic the woodpecker's head structure to improve the low-velocity impact behavior of a sandwich beam structure (which is superior to the conventional beams, with a lower developed stress, a smaller damage area and less deformation while sustaining a higher maximum impact force).441 Unlike the conventional honeycomb structure, the walls of the woodpecker's beak-inspired honeycomb structure are wavy (Fig. 22e).443 This structure enables a superior absorption capability of mechanical energy compared with 125% enlarged SEA. Besides those high SEA structures inspired by animals, structures from plants are also of interest. For example, by mimicking the composite structure of palmetto wood, bio-inspired ‘sandwich’ composite structures using carbon fiber reinforced plastic sheets and the polymeric foam core have been developed and well-investigated.444 Similar examples can be found in leaf structures,445,446 where the core is designed by filling the honeycomb into a proper ortho-grid, with improved specific strength and energy absorption. In addition, the hierarchical structures inspired by the leaf can further improve the loading capacity.447 Another hierarchical design to enhance mechanical strength could be found in hard shell animals. For example, the hierarchically structured mussel shell is typically strong and tough. As can be seen from Fig. 22f, the shell consists of micro-byssal threads. The byssal threads are in a sandwich structure at the micro-scale, made of a matrix and granules which vary in cross-linking density. The granules contain a higher cross-linking density than the matrix and this controls the difference in local mechanical properties and allows good stress dissipation and mechanical energy absorption when the shell is stressed. Inspired by this design, researchers developed a hierarchical steel composed of a hard layer and a coarse-grained layer to deliver vastly superior mechanical properties.448 This hetero-composite design may also be applied in a perovskite solar cell device. For example, a polymeric matrix is used to blend with the perovskite to form a BHJ composite, so that the polymer could (i) allow sufficient stress-energy dissipation, store the strain and consequently lead to good mechanical toughness of the composite, or (ii) allow a ‘healing process’ after damage by re-bonding the neighboring broken grains to heal the crack. Either way, from the electrical perspective, a conjugated polymer with certain electrical conductivity is of paramount importance to ensure a good PCE. Stress-tolerance for PV-MPS also includes the dynamic endurance, which means that the device needs to have a high lifetime able to handle many cycles of folding–opening–refolding. This will require a good inner friction tolerance to avoid any serious fractures of the multiple layers in the device under the condition of a large bending angle. A natural example bearing both good hardness and flexibility or mobility is the bone and joint.449 These could be attributed to the remarkable hierarchical architecture of bone, constituted by both the soft collagen protein and stiffer apatite mineral (Fig. 22g). Bone's universal elementary building block is the mineralized fibrils, which are assembled from collagen molecules and mineralized by apatite crystals. In the body, the bone tissue functionality is related to stiffness which is determined by the collagen/mineral ratio. For instance, ear bone has over 80% of mineral content so that it is sensitive and vibratile with applied acoustics. In contrast, long bone with 20% mineral content, which is light-weight to enable mobility, could absorb the mechanical energy.
Fig. 22 Other promising structures. (a) The force–displacement relationship of various energy absorbers. The shaded area under the curve represents the energy absorbed. (b) The specific energy absorption (SEA) of bio-inspired sandwich structures, adapted with permission from ref. 450, Copyright 2020 Elsevier. (c) Honeycomb sandwich structures in turtle shell and beetle forewings, adapted with permission from online resources451–453 and ref. 438, Copyright 2017 Elsevier. (d) Bio-inspired sandwich structure mimicking the shrimp, adapted with permission from ref. 450, Copyright 2020 Elsevier. (e) Photo of a woodpecker, TEM image of the rhamphotheca of a woodpecker's beak showing its nanostructure, and schematic illustration of the structure, adapted with permission from ref. 454 and 443, Copyright 2014 The Royal Society and 2019 Springer. (f) A mussel inspired hierarchical shell, adapted with permission from ref. 448, Copyright 2017 Springer Nature. (g) Hierarchical structure of bone ranging from the microscale skeleton to nanoscale collagen and hydroxyapatite, adapted with permission from ref. 455 Copyright 2013 Springer Nature.

Overall, there are various promising structures formed at ambient temperature in creatures that have specific high mechanical properties. Nevertheless, there is limited success in mimicking such functional structures for applications to improve mechanical properties for PSCs. The challenges mainly include the lack of reliable approaches for translating these ‘design motifs’ found in natural materials to a specific utilization in solar cell applications, as well as the technical challenges of making these bioinspired structures using perovskite materials that can be installed within a PV device. Nevertheless, there is exciting potential within the vast inventory of natural examples with delicate structures that may lead to inspiring design principles/rules and highly efficient functionalities implemented to minimize energy consumption. For example, preparing a nanostructured perovskite layer using the precursor ink of the perovskite and a micro-mold (e.g., PDMS mold) could be a good way to manufacture a mechanically stable and optically multifunctional film, and the mold pattern could be mimicked from the nanostructures from shrimp shell, woodpecker's beak or beetle forewings. Learning and understanding these natural designs, and eventually manufacturing and implementing them in products requires the development of advanced nanomanufacturing techniques, which can be a long-term, costly project, but may have a ground-breaking impact to the broader community outside of the PV field. Overall, nature provides a huge library of ‘design manuscripts’; how to utilize them to optimize existing models and even make innovations, and the challenges in developing more advanced tools are the two prior problems to be addressed now.

5.2 Self-cleaning

For real applications of PV-MPS, weather conditions such as rain, snow, wind, and dust need to be taken into consideration. A device with a self-cleaning feature could ensure a stable power output and extend its lifetime. In the following section, several natural prototypes for self-cleaning and corresponding self-cleaning solar cells will be discussed.
5.2.1 Lotus effect. Lotus leaves possess special water repellency and surface non-wettability where water droplets cannot stably adhere to the surface of leaves (or petals in some cases) but spontaneously roll away or bounce off. In this way, dirt particles and dust can be routinely removed from the leaves or petals, which is usually called the ‘lotus effect’ or ‘self-cleaning lotus’ (Fig. 23a).456,457 This non-wetting property originates from the nano- and microscale hierarchical cilium-like structures superimposed on top of micro-papillae, as well as an epicuticular covering of low surface energy wax on these papillae (Fig. 23a(ii) and (iii)). This structural topography at the surface with a significantly large surface area could generate a higher energy barrier at the interface between the liquid and solid, but also trap the air below the water droplets forcing them to partially sit on a membrane of air. These effects jointly give rise to a superhydrophobic property at the surface (contact angle >150° and a sliding angle <5°).456,458 The super-hydrophobicity of these solid surfaces is crucial for self-cleaning of glasses, anti-icing and prevention of snow adhesion to glass surfaces, and anti-corrosion/anti-biofouling.459
Fig. 23 Lotus-inspired structures for self-cleaning. (a) (i) Image of a water droplet on a lotus leaf, adapted from an online resource.464 (ii) and (iii) SEM image of the lotus leaf surface, adapted with permission from ref. 456, Copyright 2008 Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH & Co. KGaA. (b) (i) Schematic illustration and SEM images of the PDMS hierarchical pyramid arrays. (ii) Schematic illustration of the self-cleaning effect on the hierarchical pyramid PDMS surface. (iii) Static contact angles (green line) and roll-off angles (blue line) of water on three different surfaces, adapted with permission from ref. 458, Copyright 2014 The Royal Society of Chemistry. (c) (i) Schematic illustration of the nanocone PDMS film attached on the top of a perovskite solar cell. (ii) Self-cleaning experiment of the perovskite solar cell devices with and without the nanocone PDMS film. (iii) Contact angles of a water droplet on the structured PDMS layer with different aspect ratios, adapted with permission from ref. 147, Copyright 2015 American Chemical Society. (d) (i) SEM image of the inverted micro-pyramidal structured PDMS layer (IMPS-PDMS). (ii) Sequential photographs of the water droplet cleaning behaviour of the IMPS-PDMS film with fluoro-octyltrichlorosilane (FOTS) treatment. (iii) Photographs of a water droplet on the surface of IMPS-PDMS samples before and after the FOTS surface modification, adapted with permission from ref. 462. Copyright 2016 The Royal Society of Chemistry.
5.2.2 Lotus-inspired PSCs. This self-cleaning property is an important attribute for practical usage of solar cells, particularly for applications in remote desert areas (e.g., Africa, the Middle East, four-season countries460), some industrial areas (e.g., with accumulation of dust, snowfall and airborne dirt from factories) and solar-driven robotic spatial exploration (e.g., The Mars 2020 Perseverance Rover mission of NASA's Mars Exploration Program461). The accumulation of particles from the surrounding environment on the surface will block the light and compromise the output power of PV cells/panels/modules. Currently, cleaning methods to remove these particles include the electrostatic method, mechanical method, and coating method for PV product surface cleaning.460 Considering the energy for the cleaning process, the coating method with a specific surface microstructure design is of great interest. In the case of PSCs, Kang et al.458 reported on the water-repellent PSCs based on PDMS films with lotus leaf-inspired hierarchical arrays well-equipped with a self-cleaning capability. The structured PDMS film is additionally attached (due to the strong van der Waals interaction between PDMS and glass) to the glass side of a PSC which can enhance the light incidence by an anti-reflection effect while having a self-cleaning functionality. Fig. 23b(i) shows the scanning electron microscopy (SEM) images of the PDMS layer with hierarchical pyramid arrays (having a 45° angle at the bottom edge side). This hierarchically structured PDMS layer creates super-hydrophobicity on the surfaces leading to a self-cleaning effect (Fig. 23b(ii)), where the dust particles could be removed easily by water droplets rolling off the surface due to the large contact angle of 157° (Fig. 23b(iii)). This additional lotus-inspired PDMS thin sticker layer thus exhibits an easy but effective way of making self-cleaning PSCs. Similarly, Tavakoli et al.147 also reported a nano-cone array of an antireflection film attached onto the front electrode of the substrate in order to improve the water-repellence as well as the optical transmittance. The PDMS film (thickness of 0.2 mm) with a nanocone array pattern (1 μm pitch and 1 μm depth, as shown in Fig. 23c(i)) was fabricated by molding from an imprinted Al foil template which was first prepared via a multistep anodization coupled with a wet etching process. Fig. 23c(ii) shows the ‘dust self-cleaning experiments’, where the dust/small particles can be easily removed from the device by rolling water droplets across the surface sufficiently cleaning the dusty surface. Furthermore, the aspect ratios (defined by the height of nanocones over periodicity) of the PDMS nanocones can be tuned through the molding process. Besides the inverted micro-pyramidal structured (IMPS) PDMS coating film, incorporation of an additional hydrophobic layer can further enhance the self-cleaning effect for the device. Dudem et al.462 reported the fluoro-octyltrichlorosilane (FOTS)-coating on the surface of micro-pyramidal PDMS (Fig. 23d(i)) and obtained a superhydrophobic behavior with a very large water contact angle of 150° (Fig. 23d(iii)). The micro-pyramidal structured PDMS was prepared by peeling from a Si-mold after curing at 75 °C for 2 hours, followed by a treatment (rinse) of a mixture of trichloro(1H,1H,2H,2H-perfluorooctyl) silane and n-hexane for several minutes and drying in an oven at 30 °C for 12 hours. The self-cleaning behavior of a FOTS-treated IMPS–PDMS film was tested by dropping water on the dust covered film. Fig. 23d(ii) shows the efficient self-cleaning process. In real outdoor conditions, these nano/micro-structured PDMS films with a super-hydrophobic surface have exhibited an efficient self-cleaning ability when exposed to raindrops, and the PSCs integrated with the PDMS layer showed good device stability for 32 days.463

5.3 Self-healing

5.3.1 Self-healing in nature. ‘Healing’ is defined as the act or process of curing or of restoring to health.465 In nature, healing is an intrinsic ability in the biological world, encompassing from plants to animal kingdoms, roughly classified into eight prevalent mechanisms: (i) reversible muscle control, (ii) clotting, (iii) cellular response, (iv) layering, (v) protective surfaces, (vi) vascular networks or capsules, (vii) exposure, and (viii) replenishable functional coatings466 by the biological community. Table 7 summarizes the healing in nature (flora and fauna) through an organizational approach. Flora includes herbaceous plants that die down to ground and regrow each year (i.e., annuals, biennials, and perennials) and woody plants referring to trees that maintain a persistent woody stem above ground year-round; fauna includes vertebrates (animals with a spinal cord) and invertebrates (without a spinal cord). Due to the huge variability of species, their specific self-healing mechanisms differ significantly. For example, Fig. 24a shows the wound healing in a tree limb, where the cells at the wound (cut) are hardening to seal off the wound to prevent the exposure to harmful microbes. Some plants also have special secretion cells (or the vascularized networks throughout their tissues), which could release latex, gum or volatile oils at the cut to heal injuries and/or to repel predators.467,468Fig. 24b shows a latex producing tree as an example, which has inspired the application of ‘self-healing elastomers for dampers’.469–473 In vertebrates, bone remodeling via a complex series of cellular and humoral activities is important to the species.474Fig. 24c shows this process, which begins with the debris cleaning at the existing bone fracture by the osteoclasts (cells that break down bone tissue), followed by remodeling by pre-osteoblasts to wound the site, reproduction of the extracellular matrix and mineralization to produce new bone. During this process, continuous ‘precursor’ supply of hematopoietic and mesenchymal stem cells is crucial to this ‘synthetic reaction’. This concept has inspired ‘self-sealing and self-healing concrete’.475 As a broad overview, Table 8 lists a chronological overview of some self-healing/repair materials/applications inspired by nature according to prior literature.
Table 7 Self-healing/defense mechanisms found in living nature (partially referred from multiple references466)
Flora & fauna Healing activities (physical change) Healing/defence description Mechanism(s) Ref.
Plants Herbaceous plants Wound closure Seal-off and grow (production and maturation of callus parenchyma) Cells swell, divide, and harden at the surface 483
Secretions after injury Latex, gum, or volatile oils are released to aid healing upon breach Punctured cell induces a localized response 467 and 468
Innate immune response Pattern-recognition of microbe attack Cellular response for specific cell response 484
Woody plants Bark (epidermis) and roots (rhizodermis) growth Continual production of thick outer layers (tree bark and root bark) protecting inner layers Continual replenishment of the hard, protective layers 485–487
Animals Vertebrates Central nervous system (CNS) injury response Glial cells in the extracellular matrix quickly form scar tissue to maintain homeostasis Clearing of debris and rapid system stabilization 488 and 489
Peripheral nervous system (PNS) injury Schwann cells clear the area around the axons and allow repair Clearing of debris and rapid system stabilization 488 and 489
Vertebrate (hard tissue) Bone break Cellular response: inflammation, cartilage callus formation, lamellar bone generation and remodelling A series of cellular activities (bone side stabilization, remodelling, mineralization, etc.) 474
Vertebrate (soft tissue) Wound Cellular clotting, inflammation, proliferation, and remodelling Cellular signalling from short-term clotting to long-term cell remodelling 490

In terms of perovskites, one challenge in this material is its high chemical activity with oxygen and moisture in air. Placing a halide perovskite thin film in the ambient atmosphere will introduce a gradual surface degradation (chemical impurities, surface traps, etc.) that is harmful for their use in PSCs.476,477 Removing this already degraded surface layer inspired by the ecdysis and moulting of animals (Fig. 24d) could be a potential solution. In fact, the concept of ‘surface layer removal to obtain a high-quality fresh layer’ has been widely used chemically and physically in manufacturing (e.g., surface cleaning of monocrystalline silicon wafers for chip manufacturing)478 and in research (e.g., mechanical exfoliation of 2D materials for obtaining a fresh surface479,480). For a perovskite crystal, the surface trap has significant harmful impacts on the interfacial contact. Fig. 24e shows a cross-sectional SEM image of a perovskite crystal with poor surface morphology. Properly polishing the top surface with techniques such as laser polishing or other fine lapping may help to passivate those unwanted surface defects. To prevent surface degradation of halide perovskite films, an alternative pathway could be a quick surface passivation before reaction with air. Like the wound healing process at the epidermis in soft tissues of the invertebrates, covering the reactive surface with a clotting-like ultrathin layer may be helpful in inhibiting the reaction with ambient harmful agents. This spontaneous self-surface-protection could be conceptualized either by introducing specific additives in the perovskite that can spontaneously form a robust top protecting layer on the halide perovskite481 (will be discussed in Section 5.3 – Bio-additives) or by intentionally coating a capping layer on top of the wet halide perovskite film before its reaction with air.482 Protective skin and an outer shell are widely used strategies in nature. Fig. 24f shows the examples of cell walls of plants that can protect the cell, skin epidermis that can protect the inner tissues, the exoskeleton of insects that can provide protection against harsh environmental stimuli, and marine collagens that can protect marine organisms. In comparison, for the research of perovskite solar cells, different protection strategies have been proposed (Fig. 24f). For example, a core–shell structure could protect the core perovskite lattice, an ultrathin organic coating layer on the grain could protect the crystal grain of the perovskite, and a 2D capping layer could protect the 3D perovskite layer underneath. This is particularly important for perovskite solar cells, where the surface traps account for a significant loss. Particularly in single-crystalline perovskite solar cells, since the inner bulk region is having no boundaries, surface traps could lead to a serious issue in contact with the CTL or electrodes. Passivating these surface traps or delaminating the top-rough ‘skin’ to induce a smooth inner surface during device assembly is the next hurdle to overcome. This may require sophisticated manufacturing techniques such as laser-annealing, laser polishing and flashing lamp annealing techniques.

5.3.2 Self-healing in PSCs. PSCs targeted for MPS applications may have soft and deformable attributes. However, soft elastic materials typically used for PSCs and other electronics are susceptible to puncturing and tearing, leading to mechanical failures, performance losses, and even the lead leakage from the perovskite layer. This potential failure greatly limits the use of MPS in applications such as autonomous field robotics or remote mission (e.g., spatial mission) where repairs are challenging or even impossible. Hence, soft MPS which are self-healable and functionally repairable like natural skin or nervous tissue are of great interest. In a higher-level field view of artificial self-healing materials, many nature-inspired mechanisms and materials have been proposed. These include semiconducting polymeric networks,540–542 recoverable polymers,543 ionic hydrogels,544 liquid metal (LM) microfluidics,545,546 and LM microcapsules patterned on solid lines.547 These methods can also be transferred to be used in PSCs. At this point, self-healing in PSCs still is not fully developed. For specific consideration of halide perovskites, some aspects of this material are serious issues for industrial implementation, including the environmental impact from the lead548–550 as well as the materials’ photo- and chemical-stability.551–553 The material is intrinsically metastable with significant anharmonicity in the chemical bonds and consequent dynamic disordered states within the crystallographic lattice.554 Restoring the properties from a ‘damaged’ state of the halide perovskite to its original ‘healthy’ state to prevent device failure is of great importance. As an organic–inorganic hybrid material with a ‘soft’ attribute (a much smaller Young's modulus than those of typical inorganic semiconductors such as Si and GaAs), it is possible to heal the perovskite using the aforementioned methods applied in other ‘soft’ materials such as polymers. For example, using chemical/physical treatments (e.g., exposure to degrading chemical, thermal, or light treatments) on halide perovskites,555,556 cracks and pin-holes can be merged well. Early studies have revealed that the incorporation of polyethylene glycol (PEG) additives into the MAPbI3 perovskite can revert the degraded crystal (yellow δ-phase) back to the black β-phase.557 Specifically as shown in Fig. 25a(i), by infiltrating the perovskite into a PEG scaffold, the composite film exhibits a recovery behavior after degradation upon water spray, which otherwise would display an irreversible degradation if no PEG was involved. By constructing the PEG scaffold-based PSC (Fig. 25a(ii)), a self-healing device performance has also been obtained. The PCE decays with reduced VOC and JSC upon exposure to water vapour but then returns to its original value in less than one minute (Fig. 25a(iii)). This self-healing phenomenon might originate from the excellent hygroscopicity of the PEG coupled by its strong interaction with perovskite lattice terminals. PEG has stronger interactions with water molecules than those of the perovskite and water, so the water can be retained in the PEG phase instead of diffusing into the perovskite phase; PEG can also keep the MAI (through hydrogen interactions) from escaping away through a higher volume of water spray. By putting back into a dry atmosphere, as long as the retained water molecules evaporate, the non-perovskite yellowish phase turned back to the black β-phase. Similar to polymers, small organics with proper interactions with the perovskite lattice can also assist the self-healing of the PSC. Recently, Ran et al. reported the incorporation of large-volume amines (LVAs) in the Sn-based PSC with a self-healing property. After heating or exposing to air (the Sn-based PSC is highly sensitive to heat and oxygen due to the quick oxidation of Sn2+ in FASnI3), the LVA-based device could partially restore its PCE from 60% of its initial value to 80% after being placed in a N2 atmosphere for a specific time. This self-healing effect might be due to the steric hindrance effect of the LVAs.558
Fig. 25 Self-healing perovskite solar cell against environmental degradation. (a) (i) Photographs of perovskite films with and without PEG showing color change evolution after water-spraying. (ii) Schematic illustration of the PEG scaffold structured perovskite solar cell (PPSC). (iii) J–V curves of the PPSC before and after water spray showing the self-healing feature, adapted with permission from ref. 557, Copyright 2016 Springer. (b) (i) Schematic illustration and cross-sectional SEM image showing an inverted-structure perovskite solar cell. (ii) Normalized solar-cell parameters (VOC, JSC, FF) as a function of the proton dose, ϕ. (iii) Time dependence of the normalized solar-cell parameters after termination of the proton irradiation for a proton-irradiated device (red circles) and for a reference solar cell (black dot), adapted with permission from ref. 564, Copyright 2016 Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH & Co. KGaA. (c) (i) Schematic illustration of the biasing experiment. (ii) Schematic diagrams of the evolution of the ion distribution within the perovskite layer under solar cell working conditions, adapted wither permission from ref. 567, Copyright 2017 The Royal Society of Chemistry. (d) (i) Schematic illustration of the planar structured perovskite solar cell device. (ii) Time evolution of JSC under constant illumination and after resting the device in the dark. (iii) Schematic diagrams of the proposed photocurrent degradation and self-healing mechanism, adapted with permission from ref. 564, Copyright 2016 Springer.

Similar ‘self-healing’ effects in PSCs upon exposure and termination of proton radiation have also been observed.559 This might be more important for outer space and space ship mission applications. The proton (p) radiation, together with helium (He) (resulting from galactic cosmic radiation or solar flares, with a particle flux of 103 to 108 cm−2 s−1 depending on the distance from Earth),560–562 is the major cosmic radiation source for the solar cells and MPS used in spatial missions (e.g., spaceships and satellites in the outer space). Protons with high energies (close to 1 MeV) can induce ionization and lattice displacement, and result in device damage to solar cells.563 Lang et al.559 found that the PSC exhibited compromised PCE upon proton irradiation but displayed a self-healing effect by returning the PCE to its original value after terminating the radiation (Fig. 25b(i)). This can be understood from the proton irradiation-induced displacement of hydrogen. The proton irradiation typically results in the chemical bond breakage of C–H and N–H, rendering localized in-band states (further leading to Shockley–Read–Hall (SRH) recombination) (Fig. 25b(ii)). After termination of the proton irradiation, the displaced hydrogen atoms migrate back towards the lattice and passivate the localized defects, recovering to their original state. This self-healing process is verified in the recovery of the photocurrent (Fig. 25b(iii)).

Besides the above moisture and proton radiation stimulated self-healing, another type of healing is via illumination.564 Several groups have reported such photo-induced degradation and recovery of the device PV parameters under constant solar irradiation, applied external bias and storage in the dark. In early 2015, Bag et al.565 reported the first observation of photoinduced quasi-reversibility of the performance of PSCs (MAxFA1−xPbI3). The PSC degradation under sunlight contains both thermally activated fast ion transport and lattice-expansion triggered by IR light in the solar spectrum. Interestingly, they also observed that, after keeping a previously illuminated MAPbI3 PSC in the dark for 15 min, the lost PCE of the device can be recovered when re-illuminated. Similarly, Yadav et al.566 also found that, under conditions of illumination and bias, the JSC of the PSC decreased in a reversible pathway and recovered to its initial number after being kept in the dark for a short time. The ion movement and accumulation, and the resultant interfacial change at selective contacts is concluded to be a possible mechanism restricting the PV performance recovery.566 These ion-migration induced recoverable PCE changes in PSCs have been further supported by the work of Domanski et al.567 (Fig. 25c). By in situ cooling the working PSCs and exemption of thermally induced non-reversible PCE loss, the device exhibits a significant PCE loss under continuous operation (in light) and then shows PCE recovery to its initial value after resting in the dark. As shown in Fig. 25c(ii), in the initial condition, the ionic pairs (stoichiometric anion and cation) are randomly distributed in the perovskite layer; in the non-stabilized condition (devices are exposed to light and biased to MPP) anions (vacancies) migrate to the HTL side and form a Debye layer at the interface, leaving the relatively immobile cation behind; at longer timescales over 103 s (i.e., the stabilized condition), cations migrate and form another Debye layer at the ETL side. Both Debye layers at the HTL and ETL sides are harmful for photocarrier extraction and hence lead to PCE degradation, but it is recoverable. Particularly when the PSC device is given several hours of ‘resting’ in the dark, the ionic distribution can thermodynamically return to its initial random state and thereby the device appears to be ‘fresh’ again and thus the PCE is restored. Supported by modelling and elemental depth profiling results, the slow ionic migration (timescale >103 s) induced reversible PCE losses are further verified. Based on this timescale of >103 s, the natural day/night cycles (timescale of 104 s) can provide enough time for self-healing of the device performance spontaneously. A similar recoverable behaviour of the PSC was also observed by Lee et al.568 After being exposed to 365 nm UV light for >1000 hours under inert gas (<0.5 ppm H2O), followed by one-sun soaking (allowing dispatch of stacked charges and neutralization of defects), the PSC showed PCE recovery. In parallel, a claim of ‘light-activated metastable trap-states’564 to explain the recovery behaviour of PSCs has also been documented. Nie et al.564 demonstrated that PSC degradation under constant solar illumination is due to the photocurrent loss, which can quickly self-heal within 1 min in the dark to its original value (Fig. 25d(ii)). PSC capacitance measurements and film time-resolved (TR) PL spectroscopy attribute the observed photocurrent loss to the ‘light-activated metastable trap-states’564 (Fig. 25(iii)), which further leads to the space charges at the interface (resulting in non-homogeneous electric fields interfering with charge transport in the device) as supported by both experimental data and DFT analysis. Overall, in PSCs there have been several interesting phenomena observed for ‘self-healing’: (i) two distinct time regimes of recovery: fast recovery (i.e., <1 min,564 retrieving ∼96% of its initial PCE), slower recovery (tens of minutes to an hour, i.e., 103 s,567 fully restoring the photocurrent); (ii) the photocurrent degradation can be attributed to the formation of light-activated, metastable and deep trap-states (>0.5 eV);564 (iii) both the degradation and self-healing of PSCs are highly sensitive to temperature and light,565 the former's dynamic (timescale of 103 s) being slower than that of the latter (timescale of 102 s); (iv) a VOC decrease by 40 mV has been observed and is due to the deep trap-states. To explain these behaviours, a more general and fundamental property of the perovskite material needs to be clarified, the ‘polaron’ model.569 The formation of small polarons is generally responsible for this experimental ‘self-healing’ behaviour in PSCs based on the following considerations: (i) localized polaronic states can be manifested as deep trap-states in the forbidden band, which further gives rise to the space charge accumulation and a ≤40% photocurrent reduction; (ii) the formation time of small polarons is faster than the time scales of PSC degradation; (iii) the slow degradation of the photocurrent can be attributed to the accumulation of small polarons. Considering these, Nan et al.570 provided a first-principles calculation on the light-induced photocurrent degradation and self-healing in MAPbI3 PSCs. It is demonstrated that, under photoexcitation, changes of the crystallographic lattice (due to photo-activation of site vacancies, followed by geometric relaxation) lead to the formation of partially localized excitons being trapped at the defects. In the meantime, the other excitons can be dissociated into free charges at the femtosecond scale due to their very small exciton binding energy.571,572 Hence there is the coexistence of trapped excitons and free charges. The trapped excitons can be released (to yield a recovery effect) with an activation energy of 26 meV for the I-site vacancy and 38 meV for the Pb-site vacancy in MAPbI3. Overall, the photocurrent degradation and recovery in PSCs can be determined by the competition between the trapping and release of excitons. This mechanism is more general and can be applied for similar perovskites such as FAPbI3, MAPbBr3, and FAPbBr3, but with a faster recovery dynamic.569 The optical ‘damage’ to the perovskite film has also been investigated and found in the recovery of the PL of multiple perovskite films.569,573 These observations indicate that illumination induced ‘damage’ of the perovskite can be repaired continuously.

Outside the self-healing of the perovskite crystals, another consideration can be the quarantine of leakage of the toxic lead by some self-healing encapsulation materials when the PSC is mechanically damaged. Recently, Jiang et al.574 reported a self-healing polymer-based encapsulation to reduce the lead leakage from damage. Three encapsulation methods (Fig. 26a) have been investigated coupled with realistic simulated mechanical damage (to simulate the real hail impact) as well as Pb leakage rate. The crucial factor to minimize the Pb leakage is the self-repair ability of encapsulating polymers. Fig. 26b and c compare the different encapsulation methods in terms of their effectiveness in limiting the lead leakage. Fig. 26b shows the photographs of PSC modules with different encapsulation methods before impact and Fig. 26c shows the photographs of these PSC modules after mechanical impacts (followed by two water dripping tests, the first by heating at 45 °C and a second water dripping test). Different encapsulation methods yield various degrees of perovskite decomposition. Particularly, an epoxy resin (ER)-based polymer with a Tg of ∼42 °C, when sandwiched between a PSC module and a top capping glass, can effectively reduce the rate of Pb leakage from 30 to 0.08 mg h−1 m−2 under various simulated weather conditions. Taking advantage of such ‘self-healing’ attributes from the polymeric encapsulation materials to protect the device, quarantine the lead, and even recover the partial performance loss is a promising pathway to extend the lifecycle of PSC based MPS products. On a broader scale beyond the epoxy resin, self-healing polymers involving various chemical processes (including incorporation of covalent,575–577 free-radical,578,579 and supramolecular580–582 dynamic bonds) and physical events (such as employing enhanced van der Waals interactions,583 interdigitated copolymeric morphologies – embedded, reactive, encapsulated fluids that can infiltrate into a wound and trigger sequential chemical reactions for damage repair584 and cardiovascular networks585) are providing new insights. Although artificial self-healing in versatile systems may contain complex mechanisms, self-healing in living systems involves complex cascade reaction series, the exact chemistries of some of which are far from being understood. Resembling these natural self-healing processes from learning and mimicking the reactions at the molecular scale is the major ongoing task in the community of artificial self-healing materials. Learning, transferring, and applying these techniques into the more specialized PSC field may create a big leap to self-healable MPS products.


Fig. 26 Self-quarantine strategies in PSCs. (a) Schematic illustration showing the different encapsulation methods (left) and the experimental procedure to assess the quantity of the toxic Pb leaked from a perovskite solar module that is damaged due to an external impact (for example, hail) (right). (b) and (c) Perovskite solar modules using different encapsulation methods (b) before and (c) after impact and water dripping test, adapted with permission from ref. 574, Copyright 2019 Springer.

5.4 Bio-additives

Incorporation of processing additives into the photoactive layer is a universally effective methodology to boost PV cell performance. In OPVs, small molecule additives can tune the micromorphology of the BHJ blend and hence tune multiple electronic properties;288 in PSCs, additives have been reported to have multiple effects on the device performance. There are various types of additives including salts, molecules, polymers, and even nanoparticles,46,586–588 with varied effects, including modulating crystalline features, stabilizing the perovskite phase, adjusting energy-level alignment, enhancing operational stability, eliminating hysteresis, suppressing nonradiative recombination, etc.589,590 In contrast to those inorganic additives, biomolecular additives may have additional functionalities through molecular interactions with the perovskite lattice by their specific functional chemical groups. A good example is in caffeine,591 which is the active substance in coffee. A recent study has revealed the effectiveness of incorporation of caffeine in boosting the stability of PSCs. The caffeine molecule has two CO groups (Fig. 27a(i)) with lone pair electrons that could interact with the Pb2+ from the perovskite lattice through strong Coulombic coordination, serving as a ‘molecular interlock’ between adjacent perovskite grains. Meanwhile, these molecules can serve as Lewis bases passivating the Lewis acid-type defects like undercoordinated Pb2+ in perovskites, thus reducing the trap density. This molecular interlock effect also suppresses the ion migration, yielding a superior stability for a PSC based on the n–i–p structure (Fig. 27a(ii)) to retain 90% of the initial PCE after 1700 hours when annealed at 85 °C (in contrast to 60% of the initial performance remaining from the reference blank device after 175 hours of annealing, as shown in Fig. 27a(iii)). Similarly, Zhang et al.592 introduced N719 (a dye derived from chlorophyll) into the MAPbI3 perovskite crystal. The –COOH groups from N719 were found to form complexes with the MA+ (MA+ = CH3NH3+) in the perovskite lattice (Fig. 27b(i)), which modifies the diffusion of water molecules in the materials. Theoretical calculations show a much higher migration barrier of water molecules in the N719-based perovskite. As a result, the water penetration into the perovskite layer can be greatly inhibited (Fig. 27b(ii)), and the moisture stability of the device is significantly improved. Nearly 70% of the PCE can be retained at a high RH of 60% for 400 hours (Fig. 27b(iii)). Our group also revealed that the incorporation of hydrophobic DNA–CTMA into the perovskite crystal can sufficiently improve the device stability282 by nanoscopically wrapping the perovskite nanocrystals (Fig. 27c(i)). The strong hydrophobicity of DNA–CTMA also enhances the non-wettability upon contact with water (Fig. 27c(ii)) and thereby improves the device lifetime (Fig. 27c(iii)). Recently, our group reported a ‘perovskite/perovskite’ bilayer structure. The bottom perovskite layer is a bulk layer with a typical grain size of hundreds of nanometers, while the top perovskite layer consists of perovskite–artemisinin core–shell nanocrystals. This bilayer structure is spontaneously formed by adding the artemisinin molecules (Fig. 27d(i)), which have been known as medicine molecules against malaria.581,593 Artemisinin has a unique endoperoxide ring that produces free radicals to damage the proteins of parasites and thereby cure the disease.594,595 The sesquiterpene lactone structure and alkyls make artemisinin insoluble in water, showing a hydrophobic behavior.591 As a result, the hydrophobicity from the artemisinin-incorporated MAPbI3 top layer (Fig. 27d(ii)) improves the device stability with a retained efficiency >18% which corresponds to ∼95% of the initial PCE after one-month exposure to ambient environment (Fig. 27d(iii)). Overall, by providing functional groups to anchor the terminal partially bonded atoms in the perovskite lattice, the biomolecular additives are effective in improving the stability of the crystal. In addition, the PCEs of the resultant PSCs can be improved to certain degrees mostly due to the enhanced crystalline quality. Fig. 28 summarizes the PCEs of the PSCs incorporated with multiple bio-molecular additives. The highest PCE of 20.63% comes from the device structure of FTO/TiO2/perovskite/spiro-MeOTAD/Au with the addition of DNA–CTMA into the perovskite layer. Nevertheless, biomolecules are naturally more complex than typically used artificial additives and their role in the cascade reaction series in a species can be even more complicated and delicate. Harnessing their specific chemical group as a functional agent to couple with those active terminal atoms/crystallographic sites in the perovskite lattice might be a good way to improve performance, but it remains technically inefficient and scientifically unclear when using such complex molecules to execute simple redox chemical reactions between biomolecules and the perovskite lattice. Instead, simplifying the biomolecule structures, designing an optimal structure using the knowledgebase and knowhow from aforementioned successes, and utilizing a more functional-oriented bio-additives for PSCs might be a better alternative.

(ii) Photograph showing the degradation transformation process of the perovskite film with and without the N-719 additive.
